      <p><b>Burundi</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ʊ/: 'u' in 'push'">ʊ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:En-Burundi-pronunciation.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/En-Burundi-pronunciation.ogg" class="internal" title="En-Burundi-pronunciation.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span></span>, <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ʌ/: 'u' in 'cut'">ʌ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span>-/</a></span></span>), officially the <b>Republic of Burundi</b> (<a href="/wiki/Kirundi_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Kirundi language">Kirundi</a>: <i lang="rn">Repubulika y’u Burundi</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[u.βu.ɾǔː.ndi]</a></span>; Swahili: Jamuhuri ya Burundi; French: <i lang="fr">République du Burundi</i>, <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[buʁundi]</a></span> or <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[byʁyndi]</a></span>), is a <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">landlocked country</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Rift_Valley" title="Great Rift Valley">Great Rift Valley</a> where the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a> region and <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> converge. It is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> to the east and southeast, and the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> to the west; <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a> lies along its southwestern border. The capital cities are <a href="/wiki/Gitega" title="Gitega">Gitega</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bujumbura" title="Bujumbura">Bujumbura</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-capital-change_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-capital-change-12">[11]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Twa" title="Great Lakes Twa">Twa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hutu" title="Hutu">Hutu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tutsi" title="Tutsi">Tutsi</a> peoples have lived in Burundi for at least 500 years. For more than 200 of those years, Burundi was an independent <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Burundi" title="Kingdom of Burundi">kingdom</a>, until the beginning of the 20th century, when <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Germany</a> colonised the region.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a> and <a href="/wiki/German_Revolution_of_1918%E2%80%9319" class="mw-redirect" title="German Revolution of 1918–19">Germany's defeat</a>, it ceded the territory to <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>. Both Germans and Belgians ruled Burundi and Rwanda as a European colony known as <a href="/wiki/Ruanda-Urundi" title="Ruanda-Urundi">Ruanda-Urundi</a>. Burundi and Rwanda had never been under common rule until the time of European colonisation. </p>
      <p>Burundi gained independence in 1962 and initially had a <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Burundi" title="Kingdom of Burundi">monarchy</a>, but a series of assassinations, coups and a general climate of <a href="/wiki/Congo_Crisis" title="Congo Crisis">regional instability</a> culminated in the establishment of a republic and one-party state in 1966. Bouts of <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing" title="Ethnic cleansing">ethnic cleansing</a> and ultimately two civil wars and genocides during the <a href="/wiki/Burundian_genocide_(1972)" class="mw-redirect" title="Burundian genocide (1972)">1970s</a> and again in the <a href="/wiki/Burundian_Civil_War" title="Burundian Civil War">1990s</a> resulted in hundreds of thousands of deaths and left the <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">economy undeveloped</a> and the population as one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">world's poorest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-xlix_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xlix-14">[13]</a></sup> The presidents of Rwanda and Burundi, both Hutus, died together when <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Juv%C3%A9nal_Habyarimana_and_Cyprien_Ntaryamira" title="Assassination of Juvénal Habyarimana and Cyprien Ntaryamira">their aeroplane was shot down</a> in April 1994. 2015 witnessed <a href="/wiki/2015_Burundian_unrest" class="mw-redirect" title="2015 Burundian unrest">large-scale political strife</a> as President <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Nkurunziza" title="Pierre Nkurunziza">Pierre Nkurunziza</a> opted to run for a third term in office, a <a href="/wiki/2015_Burundian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="2015 Burundian coup d'état attempt">coup attempt failed</a> and the country's <a href="/wiki/Burundian_legislative_election,_2015" class="mw-redirect" title="Burundian legislative election, 2015">parliamentary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burundian_presidential_election,_2015" class="mw-redirect" title="Burundian presidential election, 2015">presidential</a> elections were broadly criticised by members of the international community. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a> of Burundi's political system is that of a <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential</a> <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democratic</a> republic based upon a multi-party state. The President of Burundi is the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a>. There are currently 21 <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_Burundi" title="List of political parties in Burundi">registered parties in Burundi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-state_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-15">[14]</a></sup> On 13 March 1992, Tutsi coup leader <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Buyoya" title="Pierre Buyoya">Pierre Buyoya</a> established a constitution,<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated8_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated8-16">[15]</a></sup> which provided for a multi-party political process and reflected multi-party competition.<sup id="cite_ref-PH_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PH-17">[16]</a></sup> Six years later, on 6 June 1998, the constitution was changed, broadening <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Burundi" class="mw-redirect" title="National Assembly of Burundi">National Assembly</a>'s seats and making provisions for two vice-presidents. Because of the <a href="/wiki/Arusha_Accords_(Burundi)" title="Arusha Accords (Burundi)">Arusha Accord</a>, Burundi enacted a transitional government in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-unburundi_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unburundi-18">[17]</a></sup> In October 2016, Burundi informed the UN of its intention to withdraw from the <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Burundi remains primarily a rural society, with just 13.4% of the population living in urban areas in 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-7">[6]</a></sup> The population density of around 315 people per square kilometre (753 per sq&nbsp;mi) is the second highest in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-state_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-15">[14]</a></sup> Roughly 85% of the population are of <a href="/wiki/Hutu" title="Hutu">Hutu</a> ethnic origin, 15% are <a href="/wiki/Tutsi" title="Tutsi">Tutsi</a>, and fewer than 1% are indigenous <a href="/wiki/Twa" title="Twa">Twa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-20">[19]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official languages</a> of Burundi are <a href="/wiki/Kirundi_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Kirundi language">Kirundi</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, Kirundi being recognised officially as the sole national language.<sup id="cite_ref-official_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-official-21">[20]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>One of the smallest countries in Africa, Burundi's land is used mostly for subsistence agricultural and grazing, which has led to <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Habitat_loss" class="mw-redirect" title="Habitat loss">habitat loss</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-22">[21]</a></sup> As of 2005 the country was almost completely deforested, with less than 6% of its land covered by trees and over half of that being commercial plantations.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> In addition to poverty, Burundi often suffers from corruption, weak infrastructure, poor access to health and education services, and hunger.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup> Burundi is densely populated and many young people emigrate in search of opportunities elsewhere. The <a href="/wiki/World_Happiness_Report" title="World Happiness Report">World Happiness Report 2018</a> ranked the nation as the world's least happy with a rank of 156.<sup id="cite_ref-auto1_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-25">[24]</a></sup> Burundi is a member of the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_Market_for_Eastern_and_Southern_Africa" title="Common Market for Eastern and Southern Africa">Common Market for Eastern and Southern Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a>. </p>

          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Peace_agreements"><span class="tocnumber">2.6</span> <span class="toctext">Peace agreements</span></a></li>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>Modern Burundi is named after the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Burundi" title="Kingdom of Burundi">Kingdom of Burundi</a>, which ruled the region starting in the 16th century. It may ultimately derive its name from the <a href="/wiki/Ha_people" title="Ha people">Ha people</a> of the region, whose place of origin was known as Buha.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Burundi" title="History of Burundi">History of Burundi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruanda-Urundi" title="Ruanda-Urundi">Ruanda-Urundi</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Burundi is one of the few countries in Africa, along with its neighbour Rwanda among others (such as Botswana, Lesotho, and Eswatini), to be a direct territorial continuation of a pre-colonial era African state. The early history of Burundi, and especially the role and nature of the country's three dominant ethnic groups; the Twa, Hutu and Tutsi, is highly debated amongst academics.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> However, it is important to note that the nature of culture and ethnic groups is always fluid and changing. While the groups might have migrated to the area at different times and as distinctly different ethnic groups, the current distinctions are contemporary socio-cultural constructs. Initially the different ethnic groups lived together in relative peace. The first conflicts between ethnic groups can be dated back to the 17th century, when land was becoming ever more scarce because of the continuous growth in population. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Burundi">Kingdom of Burundi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Burundi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Burundi" title="Kingdom of Burundi">Kingdom of Burundi</a>
      </div> 
      <p>The first evidence of the Burundian state dates back to the late 16th century where it emerged on the eastern foothills. Over the following centuries it expanded, annexing smaller neighbours. The Kingdom of Burundi, or Urundi, in the Great Lakes region was a polity ruled by a traditional monarch with several princes beneath him; succession struggles were common.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-4">[3]</a></sup> The king, known as the <i>mwami</i> (translated as ruler) headed a princely aristocracy (<i>ganwa</i>) which owned most of the land and required a tribute, or tax, from local farmers (mainly Hutu) and herders (mainly Tutsi). The Kingdom of Burundi was characterised by a hierarchical political authority and tributary economic exchange.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the mid-18th century, the Tutsi royalty consolidated authority over land, production, and distribution with the development of the ubugabire—a patron-client relationship in which the populace received royal protection in exchange for tribute and land tenure. By this time, the royal court was made up of the Tutsi-Banyaruguru, they had higher social status than other pastoralists such as the Tutsi-Hima. In the lower levels of this society were generally Hutu people, and at the very bottom of the pyramid were the Twa. The system had some fluidity however, some Hutu people belonged to the nobility and in this way also had a say in the functioning of the state.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The classification of Hutu or Tutsi was not merely based on ethnic criteria alone. Hutu farmers that managed to acquire wealth and livestock were regularly granted the higher social status of Tutsi, some even made it to become close advisors of the <i>Ganwa</i>. On the other hand, there are also reports of Tutsi that lost all their cattle and subsequently lost their higher status and were called Hutu. Thus, the distinction between Hutu and Tutsi was also a socio-cultural concept, instead of a purely ethnic one.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-weissman_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weissman-31">[30]</a></sup> There were also many reports of marriages between Hutu and Tutsi people.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> In general, regional ties and power struggles played a far more determining role in Burundi's politics than ethnicity.<sup id="cite_ref-weissman_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weissman-31">[30]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Burundi ceased to be a monarchy when king Ntare V Ndizeye was deposed by his Prime Minister and Chief of Staff, Capt. Michel Micombero, who abolished the monarchy and declared a republic following the November 1966 coup d'état. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonisation">Colonisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Colonisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruanda-Urundi" title="Ruanda-Urundi">Ruanda-Urundi</a>
      </div> 
      <p>From 1884, the <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa_Company" title="German East Africa Company">German East Africa Company</a> was active in the African Great Lakes region. As a result of heightened tensions and border disputes between the German East Africa Company, the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Zanzibar" title="Sultanate of Zanzibar">Sultanate of Zanzibar</a>, the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> was called upon to put down the <a href="/wiki/Abushiri_revolt" title="Abushiri revolt">Abushiri revolts</a> and protect the empire's interests in the region. The German East Africa Company transferred its rights to the German Empire in 1891, in this way establishing the German colony of <a href="/wiki/German_East_Africa" title="German East Africa">German East Africa</a>, which included Burundi (Urundi), Rwanda (Ruanda), and the mainland part of <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> (formerly known as <a href="/wiki/Tanganyika_(territory)" title="Tanganyika (territory)">Tanganyika</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> The German Empire stationed armed forces in Rwanda and Burundi during the late 1880s. The location of the present-day city of <a href="/wiki/Gitega" title="Gitega">Gitega</a> served as an administrative centre for the Ruanda-Urundi region.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Campaign_(World_War_I)" class="mw-redirect" title="East African Campaign (World War I)">East African Campaign</a> greatly affected the African Great Lakes region. The <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">allied powers</a>, the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a> launched a coordinated attack on the German colony. The German army stationed in Burundi was forced to retreat by the numerical superiority of the Belgian army and by 17 June 1916, Burundi and Rwanda were occupied. The Force Publique and the British <a href="/wiki/Lake_Force" title="Lake Force">Lake Force</a> then started a thrust to capture <a href="/wiki/Tabora" title="Tabora">Tabora</a>, an administrative centre of central German East Africa. After the war, as outlined in the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a>, Germany was forced to cede "control" of the Western section of the former German East Africa to <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-state_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-15">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[34]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 20 October 1924, <a href="/wiki/Ruanda-Urundi" title="Ruanda-Urundi">Ruanda-Urundi</a>, which consisted of modern-day Rwanda and Burundi, became a Belgian <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_mandate" title="League of Nations mandate">mandate territory</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Bujumbura" title="Bujumbura">Usumbura</a> as its capital. In practical terms it was considered part of the <a href="/wiki/Belgian_colonial_empire" title="Belgian colonial empire">Belgian colonial empire</a>. Burundi, as part of Ruanda-Urundi, continued its kingship <a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">dynasty</a> despite the invasion of Europeans.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-7">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wein5_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wein5-36">[35]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Belgians, however, preserved many of the kingdom's institutions; the Burundian monarchy succeeded in surviving into the post-colonial period.<sup id="cite_ref-auto_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-4">[3]</a></sup> Following the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, Ruanda-Urundi was classified as a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Trust_Territory" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Trust Territory">United Nations Trust Territory</a> under Belgian administrative authority.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-7">[6]</a></sup> During the 1940s, a series of policies caused divisions throughout the country. On 4 October 1943, powers were split in the legislative division of Burundi's government between chiefdoms and lower chiefdoms. Chiefdoms were in charge of land, and lower sub-chiefdoms were established. Native authorities also had powers.<sup id="cite_ref-wein5_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wein5-36">[35]</a></sup> In 1948, Belgium allowed the region to form <a href="/wiki/Political_parties" class="mw-redirect" title="Political parties">political parties</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-state_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-15">[14]</a></sup> These factions contributed to Burundi gaining its independence from Belgium, on 1 July 1962. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence">Independence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Independence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Burundi_(1962%E2%80%931966).svg" class="image"><img alt="flag white saltire cross on green and red background with plant in centre white rondel" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Flag_of_Burundi_%281962%E2%80%931966%29.svg/220px-Flag_of_Burundi_%281962%E2%80%931966%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Flag_of_Burundi_%281962%E2%80%931966%29.svg/330px-Flag_of_Burundi_%281962%E2%80%931966%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Flag_of_Burundi_%281962%E2%80%931966%29.svg/440px-Flag_of_Burundi_%281962%E2%80%931966%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Burundi_(1962%E2%80%931966).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Flag of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Burundi" title="Kingdom of Burundi">Kingdom of Burundi</a> (1962–1966).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG" class="image"><img alt="flag sign at flagpole and raised plaza" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG/220px-Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG/330px-Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG/440px-Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Plaza_de_la_Independencia.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Independence Square and monument in Bujumbura.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 20 January 1959, Burundi's ruler <a href="/wiki/Mwambutsa_IV_Bangiriceng_of_Burundi" class="mw-redirect" title="Mwambutsa IV Bangiriceng of Burundi">Mwami Mwambutsa IV</a> requested Burundi's independence from Belgium and dissolution of the Ruanda-Urundi union.<sup id="cite_ref-wein7_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wein7-37">[36]</a></sup> In the following months, Burundian political parties began to advocate for the end of Belgian colonial rule and the separation of Rwanda and Burundi.<sup id="cite_ref-wein7_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wein7-37">[36]</a></sup> The first and largest of these political parties was the <a href="/wiki/Union_for_National_Progress" title="Union for National Progress">Union for National Progress</a> (UPRONA). </p>
      <p>Burundi's push for independence was influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_Revolution" title="Rwandan Revolution">Rwandan Revolution</a> and the accompanying instability and ethnic conflict that occurred there. As a result of the Rwandan Revolution, many Rwandan Tutsi refugees arrived in Burundi during the period from 1959 to 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-people60_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-people60-38">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-39">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Burundi's first <a href="/wiki/Burundian_legislative_election,_1961" class="mw-redirect" title="Burundian legislative election, 1961">elections</a> took place on 8 September 1961 and UPRONA, a multi-ethnic unity party led by <a href="/wiki/Prince_Louis_Rwagasore" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Louis Rwagasore">Prince Louis Rwagasore</a> won just over 80% of the electorate's votes. In the wake of the elections, on 13 October, the 29-year-old <a href="/wiki/Prince_Louis_Rwagasore#Assassination_in_1961" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Louis Rwagasore">Prince Rwagasore was assassinated</a>, robbing Burundi of its most popular and well-known nationalists.<sup id="cite_ref-state_15-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-15">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The country claimed independence on 1 July 1962,<sup id="cite_ref-state_15-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-15">[14]</a></sup> and legally changed its name from Ruanda-Urundi to Burundi.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup> Burundi became a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> with Mwami Mwambutsa IV, Prince Rwagasore's father, serving as the country's king.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-39">[38]</a></sup> On 18 September 1962 Burundi joined the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1963, King Mwambutsa appointed a Hutu prime minister, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Ngendandumwe" title="Pierre Ngendandumwe">Pierre Ngendandumwe</a>, but he was assassinated on 15 January 1965 by a Rwandan Tutsi employed by the US Embassy. The assassination occurred in the broader context of the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Crisis" title="Congo Crisis">Congo Crisis</a> during which <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western</a> anti-communist countries were confronting the communist <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of China">People's Republic of China</a> as it attempted to make Burundi a logistics base for communist insurgents battling in Congo.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Burundian_legislative_election,_1965" class="mw-redirect" title="Burundian legislative election, 1965">Parliamentary elections in May 1965</a> brought a majority of Hutu into the parliament, but when King Mwambutsa appointed a Tutsi prime minister, some Hutu felt this was unjust and ethnic tensions were further increased. In October 1965, an attempted <a href="/wiki/1965_Burundian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" class="mw-redirect" title="1965 Burundian coup d'état">coup d'état</a> led by the Hutu-dominated police was carried out but failed. The Tutsi dominated army, then led by Tutsi officer Captain <a href="/wiki/Michel_Micombero" title="Michel Micombero">Michel Micombero</a><sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-39">[38]</a></sup> purged Hutu from their ranks and carried out reprisal attacks which ultimately claimed the lives of up to 5,000 people in a precursor to the <a href="/wiki/Ikiza" title="Ikiza">1972 Burundian Genocide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>King Mwambutsa, who had fled the country during the October coup of 1965, was deposed by a <a href="/wiki/July_1966_Burundian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="July 1966 Burundian coup d'état">coup in July 1966</a> and his teenage son, <a href="/wiki/Ntare_V_of_Burundi" title="Ntare V of Burundi">Prince Ntare V</a>, claimed the throne. In November that same year, the Tutsi Prime Minister, then-Captain Michel Micombero, carried out another <a href="/wiki/November_1966_Burundian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="November 1966 Burundian coup d'état">coup</a>, this time deposing Ntare, abolishing the monarchy and declaring the nation a <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>, though his one-party government was effectively a <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship" title="Military dictatorship">military dictatorship</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-state_15-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-state-15">[14]</a></sup> As president, Micombero became an advocate of <a href="/wiki/African_socialism" title="African socialism">African socialism</a> and received support from the People's Republic of China. He imposed a staunch regime of law and order and sharply repressed Hutu militarism. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_war_and_genocides">Civil war and genocides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Civil war and genocides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Burundian_genocide_(1972)" class="mw-redirect" title="Burundian genocide (1972)">Burundian genocide (1972)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burundian_genocide_(1993)" class="mw-redirect" title="Burundian genocide (1993)">Burundian genocide (1993)</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In late April 1972, two events led to the outbreak of the busu famine <a href="/wiki/Burundian_genocide_(1972)" class="mw-redirect" title="Burundian genocide (1972)">First Burundian Genocide</a>. On 27 April 1972, a rebellion led by <a href="/wiki/Hutu" title="Hutu">Hutu</a> members of the <a href="/wiki/Gendarmerie" title="Gendarmerie">gendarmerie</a> broke out in the lakeside towns of <a href="/wiki/Rumonge" title="Rumonge">Rumonge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nyanza-Lac" class="mw-redirect" title="Nyanza-Lac">Nyanza-Lac</a> and the rebels declared the short-lived <a href="/wiki/Martyazo_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Martyazo Republic">Martyazo Republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup> The rebels attacked both Tutsi and any Hutu who refused to join their rebellion.<sup id="cite_ref-totten1_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-totten1-48">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> During this initial Hutu outbreak, anywhere from 80 to 120 people were killed.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup> At the same time, King <a href="/wiki/Ntare_V_of_Burundi" title="Ntare V of Burundi">Ntare V of Burundi</a> returned from exile, heightening political tension in the country. On 29 April 1972, the 24-year-old Ntare V was murdered. In subsequent months, the Tutsi-dominated government of <a href="/wiki/Michel_Micombero" title="Michel Micombero">Michel Micombero</a> used the army to combat Hutu rebels and commit genocide, murdering targeted members of the Hutu majority. The total number of casualties was never established, but contemporary estimates put the number of people killed between 298,000 and 370,000.<sup id="cite_ref-white_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white-51">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ICIBFR-85_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICIBFR-85-52">[51]</a></sup> In addition, several hundred thousand Hutu were estimated to have fled the killings into <a href="/wiki/Za%C3%AFre" class="mw-redirect" title="Zaïre">Zaïre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ICIBFR-85_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICIBFR-85-52">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> == Following the civil war and genocide, Micombero became mentally distraught and withdrawn. In 1976, Colonel <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Bagaza" title="Jean-Baptiste Bagaza">Jean-Baptiste Bagaza</a>, a Tutsi, led a <a href="/wiki/1976_Burundian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1976 Burundian coup d'état">bloodless coup</a> to topple Micombero and set about promoting reform. His administration drafted a new constitution in 1981, which maintained Burundi's status as a <a href="/wiki/One-party_state" title="One-party state">one-party state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-39">[38]</a></sup> In August 1984, Bagaza was elected head of state. During his tenure, Bagaza suppressed political opponents and religious freedoms. </p>
      <p>Major <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Buyoya" title="Pierre Buyoya">Pierre Buyoya</a> (Tutsi) <a href="/wiki/1987_Burundian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1987 Burundian coup d'état">overthrew Bagaza in 1987</a>, suspended the constitution and dissolved political parties. He reinstated military rule by a Military Committee for National Salvation (CSMN).<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_39-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-39">[38]</a></sup> Anti-Tutsi ethnic propaganda disseminated by the remnants of the 1972 UBU, which had re-organized as PALIPEHUTU in 1981, led to killings of Tutsi peasants in the northern communes of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ntega&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ntega (page does not exist)">Ntega</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Marangara&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Marangara (page does not exist)">Marangara</a> in August 1988. The government put the death toll at 5,000<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>; some international NGOs believed this understated the deaths. </p>
      <p>The new regime did not unleash the harsh reprisals of 1972. Its effort to gain public trust was eroded when it decreed an <a href="/wiki/Amnesty" title="Amnesty">amnesty</a> for those who had called for, carried out, and taken credit for the killings. Analysts have called this period the beginning of the "culture of impunity." Other analysts put the origins of the "culture of impunity" earlier, in 1965 and 1972, when a small number of identifiable Hutus unleashed massive killings of Tutsis.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>In the aftermath of the killings, a group of Hutu intellectuals wrote an open letter to <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Buyoya" title="Pierre Buyoya">Pierre Buyoya</a>, asking for more representation of the Hutu in the administration. They were arrested and jailed. A few weeks later, Buyoya appointed a new government, with an equal number of Hutu and Tutsi ministers. He appointed <a href="/wiki/Adrien_Sibomana" title="Adrien Sibomana">Adrien Sibomana</a> (Hutu) as Prime Minister. Buyoya also created a commission to address issues of national unity.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_39-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-39">[38]</a></sup> In 1992, the government created a new constitution that provided for a multi-party system,<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_39-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-39">[38]</a></sup> but a <a href="/wiki/Civil_war" title="Civil war">civil war</a> broke out. </p>
      <p>An estimated total of 250,000 people died in Burundi from the various conflicts between 1962 and 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> Since Burundi's independence in 1962, two <a href="/wiki/Burundi_genocide_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Burundi genocide (disambiguation)">genocides</a> have taken place in the country: the 1972 mass killings of Hutus by the Tutsi-dominated army,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> and the mass killings of Tutsis in 1993 by the Hutu majority. Both were described as genocides in the final report of the International Commission of Inquiry for Burundi presented in 2002 to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ICIBFR-85-496_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICIBFR-85-496-56">[55]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_attempt_at_democracy_and_war_between_Tutsi_National_Army_and_Hutu_population">First attempt at democracy and war between Tutsi National Army and Hutu population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: First attempt at democracy and war between Tutsi National Army and Hutu population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>In June 1993, <a href="/wiki/Melchior_Ndadaye" title="Melchior Ndadaye">Melchior Ndadaye</a>, leader of the Hutu-dominated <a href="/wiki/Front_for_Democracy_in_Burundi" title="Front for Democracy in Burundi">Front for Democracy in Burundi</a> (FRODEBU), won the first democratic election. He became the first Hutu head of state, leading a pro-Hutu government. Though he moved to attempt to smooth the country's bitter ethnic divide, his reforms antagonised soldiers in the Tutsi-dominated army, and he was assassinated amidst a failed military coup in October 1993, after only three months in office. The ensuing <a href="/wiki/Burundian_Civil_War" title="Burundian Civil War">Burundian Civil War</a> (1993–2005) saw persistent violence between Hutu rebels and the Tutsi majority army. It is estimated that some 300,000 people, mostly civilians, were killed in the years following the assassination.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In early 1994, the parliament elected <a href="/wiki/Cyprien_Ntaryamira" title="Cyprien Ntaryamira">Cyprien Ntaryamira</a> (Hutu) to the office of president. He and <a href="/wiki/Juv%C3%A9nal_Habyarimana" title="Juvénal Habyarimana">Juvénal Habyarimana</a>, the president of Rwanda, both Hutus, died together <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Habyarimana_and_Ntaryamira" class="mw-redirect" title="Assassination of Habyarimana and Ntaryamira">when their aeroplane was shot down</a> in April 1994. More refugees started fleeing to Rwanda. Speaker of Parliament, <a href="/wiki/Sylvestre_Ntibantunganya" title="Sylvestre Ntibantunganya">Sylvestre Ntibantunganya</a> (Hutu), was appointed as president in October 1994. A coalition government involving 12 of the 13 parties was formed. A feared general massacre was averted, but violence broke out. A number of Hutu refugees in Bujumbura,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> the then-capital, were killed. The mainly Tutsi Union for National Progress withdrew from the government and parliament. </p>
      <p>In 1996, <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Buyoya" title="Pierre Buyoya">Pierre Buyoya</a> (Tutsi) again took power through a <a href="/wiki/1996_Burundian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1996 Burundian coup d'état">coup d'état</a>. He suspended the constitution and was sworn in as president in 1998. This was the start of his second term as president, after his first term from 1987 to 1993. In response to rebel attacks, the government forced much of the population to move to <a href="/wiki/Refugee_camp" title="Refugee camp">refugee camps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup> Under Buyoya's rule, long peace talks started, mediated by <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>. Both parties signed agreements in <a href="/wiki/Arusha" title="Arusha">Arusha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pretoria" title="Pretoria">Pretoria</a>, South Africa, to share power in Burundi. The agreements took four years to plan. </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Second_Congo_War_Africa_map_en.svg" class="image"><img alt="color map of African countries showing Uganda Rwanda and Burundi backing rebels against Kabila" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Second_Congo_War_Africa_map_en.svg/220px-Second_Congo_War_Africa_map_en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Second_Congo_War_Africa_map_en.svg/330px-Second_Congo_War_Africa_map_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Second_Congo_War_Africa_map_en.svg/440px-Second_Congo_War_Africa_map_en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1525" data-file-height="1440"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Second_Congo_War_Africa_map_en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Belligerents of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Congo_War" title="Second Congo War">Second Congo War</a>. Burundi backed the rebels.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 28 August 2000, a transitional government for Burundi was planned as a part of the Arusha Peace and Reconciliation Agreement. The transitional government was placed on a trial basis for five years. After several aborted cease-fires, a 2001 peace plan and power-sharing agreement has been relatively successful. A cease-fire was signed in 2003 between the Tutsi-controlled Burundian government and the largest Hutu rebel group, CNDD-FDD (National Council for the Defense of Democracy-Forces for the Defense of Democracy).<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 2003, FRODEBU leader <a href="/wiki/Domitien_Ndayizeye" title="Domitien Ndayizeye">Domitien Ndayizeye</a> (Hutu) was elected president.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> In early 2005, ethnic quotas were formed for determining positions in Burundi's government. Throughout the year, elections for parliament and president occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Pierre_Nkurunziza" title="Pierre Nkurunziza">Pierre Nkurunziza</a> (Hutu), once a leader of a rebel group, was elected president in 2005. As of 2008<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the Burundian government was talking with the Hutu-led Palipehutu-National Liberation Forces (NLF)<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> to bring peace to the country.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peace_agreements">Peace agreements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Peace agreements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <p>African leaders began a series of peace talks between the warring factions following a request by the United Nations Secretary General <a href="/wiki/Boutros_Boutros-Ghali" title="Boutros Boutros-Ghali">Boutros Boutros-Ghali</a> for them to intervene in the humanitarian crisis. Talks were initiated under the aegis of former Tanzanian President <a href="/wiki/Julius_Nyerere" title="Julius Nyerere">Julius Nyerere</a> in 1995; following his death, South African President <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a> took the helm. As the talks progressed, South African President <a href="/wiki/Thabo_Mbeki" title="Thabo Mbeki">Thabo Mbeki</a> and United States President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> also lent their respective weight. </p>
      <p>The peace talks took the form of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Track_I_mediation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Track I mediation (page does not exist)">Track I mediations</a>. This method of negotiation can be defined as a form of diplomacy involving governmental or intergovernmental representatives, who may use their positive reputations, mediation or the "carrot and stick" method as a means of obtaining or forcing an outcome, frequently along the lines of "bargaining" or "win-lose".<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The main objective was to transform the Burundian government and military structurally in order to bridge the ethnic gap between the Tutsi and Hutu. It was to take place in two major steps. First, a transitional power-sharing government would be established, with the presidents holding office for three-year terms. The second objective involved a restructuring of the armed forces, where the two groups would be represented equally. </p>
      <p>As the protracted nature of the peace talks demonstrated, the mediators and negotiating parties confronted several obstacles. First, the Burundian officials perceived the goals as "unrealistic" and viewed the treaty as ambiguous, contradictory and confusing. Second, and perhaps most importantly, the Burundians believed the treaty would be irrelevant without an accompanying cease fire. This would require separate and direct talks with the rebel groups. The main Hutu party was sceptical of the offer of a power-sharing government; they alleged that they had been deceived by the Tutsi in past agreements. </p>
      <p>In 2000,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> the Burundian President signed the treaty, as well as 13 of the 19 warring Hutu and Tutsi factions. Disagreements persisted over which group would preside over the nascent government, and when the ceasefire would begin. The spoilers of the peace talks were the hardliner Tutsi and Hutu groups who refused to sign the accord; as a result, violence intensified. Three years later at a summit of African leaders in Tanzania, the Burundian president and the main opposition Hutu group signed an accord to end the conflict; the signatory members were granted ministerial posts within the government. However, smaller militant Hutu groups – such as the Forces for National Liberation – remained active. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="UN_involvement">UN involvement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Burundi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: UN involvement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <table class="box-Multiple_issues plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-multiple_issues compact-ambox" role="presentation">

            </div> 
           </div><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small>
          </div></td>
